Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/medium/am2c15359_0010.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15359&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">The relatively large ionic radius of the Na ion is one of the primary reasons for the slow diffusion of Na ions compared to that of Li ions in de/intercalation processes in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Interlayer expansion of intercalation hosts is one of the effective techniques for facilitating Na-ion diffusion. For most ionic layered compounds, interlayer expansion relies on intercalation of guest ions. It is important to investigate the role of these ions for material development of SIBs. In this study, alkali-metal ions (Li<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, and Cs<sup>+</sup>) with different sizes were intercalated into lepidocrocite-type layered titanate by a simple ion-exchange technique to achieve interlayer modulation and those were then evaluated as anode materials for SIBs. By controlling the intercalated alkaline ion species, basal spacings of layered titanates (LTs) in the range of 0.68 to 0.85 nm were obtained. Interestingly, the largest interlayer spacing induced by the large size of Cs did not yield the best performance, while the Na intercalated layered titanate (Na-ILT) demonstrated a superior performance with a specific capacity of 153 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. We found that the phenomena can be explained by the high alkaline metal ion concentration and the efficient utilization of the active sites in Na-ILT. The detailed analysis indicates that large intercalating ions like Cs can hamper sodium-ion diffusion although the interlayer spacing is large. Our work suggests that adopting an appropriate interlayer ion species is key to developing highly efficient layered electrode materials for SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D5028%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DSajid%2BAli%252C%2BYanyan%2BZhang%252C%2BHaoyuan%2BYang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D4%26contentID%3Dacsami.2c15359%26title%3DAltering%2Bthe%2BAlkaline%2BMetal%2BIons%2Bin%2BLepidocrocite-Type%2BLayered%2BTitanate%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D5037%26publicationDate%3DFebruary%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion"> Diffusion </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291688&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layered materials"> Layered materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Layered+Titanate&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_layered%20titanate">layered titanate</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Interlayer+Ions&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_interlayer%20ions">interlayer ions</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Interlayer+Distance&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_interlayer%20distance">interlayer distance</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrochemical+Performance&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrochemical%20performance">electrochemical performance</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Because of the abundant supply and low cost of Na, as well as their functioning mechanisms similar to those of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been investigated as a potential replacement for presently commercialized LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> Because of its larger ionic radius, higher mass, and smaller operating voltage, the Na ion puts several limits on battery application compared to the Li ion. During intercalation–deintercalation processes, the larger size of the Na ion (1.02 Å) compared to that of the Li ion (0.76 Å) results in a lower diffusion rate and very serious distortion of the electrode structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a> The adverse compatibility between large intercalating ions and sterically restricted interlayer distances in host substances cause the poor rates and sustainability of Na-ion storage materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> An increase in the interlayer distance of host materials is therefore a promising strategy to provide a solution for large-ion storage (e.g., Na ions). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Numerous studies have shown that changing the lengths of intercalated <i>n</i>-alkylamine molecules regulates the interlayer distance of layered inorganic materials and the modification in the interlayer distance can increase the electrode performance of layered materials, e.g., MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 8 to 16">(8−16)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Modification of the interlayer distance of ionic layered materials is easy to achieve by the cation-exchange technique, and various layered inorganic materials were employed as the host for the Na ion to acquire effective SIB anode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 17 to 22">(17−22)</a> Layered titanate (LT) is one of the well-studied ionic layered materials and very attractive for SIB research due to its excellent stability, low redox potential, low cost, ease of processing, and lack of toxicity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 23 to 31">(23−31)</a> Significantly, the microstructure of LT can be modified across a large range by a simple solution process. This enables a thorough investigation of the impact of interlayer spacing and other important parameters on electrode performance for developing layered SIB anode materials. Piao et al. demonstrated that intercalation of <i>n</i>-alkylamine with different chain lengths can drastically change the sodium-ion storage performance by adopting suitable chemical environments for sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Shirpour et al. studied the sodium-ion storage performance of a layered lepidocrocite-type titanate (K<sub>0.8</sub>T<sub>1.73</sub>Li<sub>0.27</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) and its sodium form (Na<sub>0.8</sub>T<sub>1.73</sub>Li<sub>0.27</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) and found that the replacement of K by Na greatly enhances the capacity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Markus et al. studied LT materials as the anode material for SIBs by combination of experimental and computational approaches and demonstrated the anisotropic nature of sodium-ion diffusion in LT, and sodium-ion diffusion is favorable along the <i>c</i> axis compared to that along the <i>a</i> axis. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Even though enlarging the interlayer distance of layered materials has been found to play an important role in promoting the performance of SIBs, the role of interlayer ions remains an obstacle to further understand layered materials for developing SIBs. In this work, various alkali-metal ions (Li, Na, K, and Cs ions) were used as intercalating agents by a simple solution process to study the role of interlayer ions in LT as SIB anode electrodes. The obtained composites have similar morphologies and crystallinities, allowing a clear study on the impact of interlayer ion species on Na storage capacity, cycle stability, and ion diffusivity. The results show that the Na intercalated sample delivers the highest specific capacity among all samples, even though its interlayer spacing is not the largest.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis of Alkaline Metal Ion Intercalated Layered Titanate</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Synthesis of K<sub>0.8</sub>L<sub>0.27</sub>T<sub>1.73</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (KLTO) and H<sub>1.07</sub>T<sub>1.73</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (HTO) crystals was conducted following our previous protocol. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> In simple terms, KLTO material was obtained by simple calcination of a mixture of K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and TiO<sub>2</sub>, and the obtained KLTO crystals were immersed in HCl solution at room temperature for 72 h with stirring. One gram of KLTO was dissolved in 100 mL of 1 mol/L HCl solution. On a daily basis, the acid solution was refreshed. Finally, HTO material was obtained after treating with a considerable quantity of distilled water, filtration, and drying. Then, the HTO crystals were immersed in alkaline hydroxide solution (Li, Na, K, and Cs) over 7 days to produce alkaline metal ion intercalated layered titanates (AMIILTs). 100 mL of hydroxide solution (1 mol/L) was used to treat 1 g of HTO particles. Then, the four types of AMIILT particles were washed, filtrated, and dried at room temperature (Li, Na, K, and Cs ion-exchange LTs are represented as Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT, respectively). After this, Li, Na, Cs, and K intercalated samples were heated at 200 and 400 °C for 5 h to remove the interlayer water.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure of the samples was analyzed by carrying out powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis with a Philips X’Pert diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.5418 Å). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to determine the chemical state of the samples (Kratos, Axis Ultra<sup>DLD</sup>), and the adventitious carbon peak located at 284.8 eV was used to calibrate the raw XPS data. The morphology and microstructure of the samples were analyzed by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM; Zeiss EVO HD 15) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM; Jeol, JEM-2100). The elemental composition was determined using inductively coupled plasma–mass spectrometry (ICP–MS) analysis (Thermo iCAP PRO).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">By combining the active materials with acetylene black as well as carboxymethylcellulose (CMC) at a weight ratio of 70:20:10, a homogeneous slurry was made, and then the slurry was coated onto a Cu foil to prepare working electrodes of the samples. Furthermore, the working electrodes were dried at 80 °C for 4 h and then at 120 °C for over 12 h inside a vacuum oven. The mass density of the working electrodes was controlled to be ∼1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. A half-cell built in a CR2032 cell was used to assess the electrochemical performance. The electrolyte, separator, and counter electrode were 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> (diglyme), glass fiber (Whatman GF/C), and a metallic Na disk, respectively. The whole half-cell assembly procedure took place in an argon-filled glovebox with oxygen and moisture levels below 0.1 ppm. A multichannel battery test system (Neware) was used to perform galvanostatic discharge and charge cycles at various rates utilizing a voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. On an electrochemical workstation (VMP3), cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were made in the voltage range of 0.01 to 3.0 V. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed in the frequency range of 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz before and after electrochemical cycling.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Calculation Details</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed using the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP), within the projector augmented-wave approach with Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> generalized gradient approximation (GGA). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The DFT-D3 method <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> was adopted to treat the van der Waals interactions. A plane wave basis set cutoff energy of 400 eV was used, and the <i>k</i>-point grid was 3 × 3× 3 in the Brillouin zones. Structure optimization was performed until the force on each atom was less than 0.02 eV/Å, and the total energy was converged up to 10<sup>–4</sup> eV. The supercell used in this work contained 155 atoms. The alkaline ions were distributed into the AMIILT layers at an atomic ratio of alkaline ion A:Ti = 2:9. The climbing image-nudged elastic band (NEB) method <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> was adopted to calculate the kinetic energy barriers during Na-ion migrations in AMIILT layers. The formation energy was calculated as <i>E</i><sub>formation</sub> = <i>E</i><sub>total</sub><sub>(Na-AMIILT)</sub> – <i>E</i><sub>total (Na)</sub> – <i>E</i><sub>total (AMIILT)</sub>. The redox mechanism of Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> coupling is responsible for charge storage in all AMIILT samples. Assuming that 70% of Ti<sup>4+</sup> sites were available for the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction, then the theoretical capacity of all samples was calculated by using the equation <i>Q</i> = <i>nF</i>/<i>M</i>, where <i>n</i> is the number of electrons transferring, <i>F</i> is Faraday’s constant, and <i>M</i> is the molecular mass. The ZSimpWin software (version 1.0.0.0) was used to analyze the EIS data.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.1.  Structural and Morphological Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The powder XRD of AMIILTs is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a. All AMIILTs have layered structures. According to the size of alkali-metal ions, the diffraction peak position of the (020) plane of the samples moved from a higher to a lower angle. The formation of well-ordered intercalation structures with various alkali-metal ions is supported by the continuous expansion of the interlayer spacing as the ionic radius of the guest molecules increases. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> The basal spacings of Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT are calculated by using the position of the (020) peak, and their values are 0.68, 0.71, 0.76, and 0.85 nm at angles of 12.9, 12.4, 11.6, and 10.4°, respectively. These data are similar to our previous findings. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The interlayer distance changes from 0.68 to 0.85 nm as the size of the interlayer ion increases. The diffraction peak at 10.4° marked by a dot in Na-ILT is due to its swollen phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Sasaki et al. also noticed the swollen phase of Na-ILT and suggested that the swollen phase is responsible for the formation of a bilayer hydrate, as opposed to other monolayer hydrates. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/medium/am2c15359_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Powder XRD patterns (the black square in Na-ILT represents its swelling phase), (b) XPS survey spectra, (c) Ti 2p XPS spectra, and (d) O 1s XPS spectra of Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT. The circle-pointed line in panels (c) and (d) represents the experimental results, while the other lines represent the fitting outcomes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15359&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">XPS was used to characterize the chemical state of each LT. The XPS survey spectra shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b prove the existence of Ti, O, and the corresponding alkaline metal ions in each sample. In the high-resolution XPS spectrum of Ti 2p (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c), a pair of peaks centered at 458.4, 458.2, 458.3, and 458.4 eV for 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 464.1, 463.8, 464.0, and 464.2 eV for 2p<sub>1/2</sub> are observed for Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT, respectively. The positions of these peaks are very close to each other, and the peaks can be assigned to Ti<sup>4+</sup> in bulk form. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 38 to 41">(38−41)</a> Besides these, two additional peaks centered at ∼459.1 eV for 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and ∼465.5 eV for 2p<sub>1/2</sub> are present in all LTs, and these peaks are identical to those of Ti<sup>4+</sup> in titanate nanosheets, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> suggesting the presence of a small amount of nanosized titanate. The O 1s peak in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d was fitted with three peaks for Li-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT and with four peaks for Na-ILT. For Li-ILT, these peaks are at 529.8, 530.5, and 531.6 eV; for Na-ILT, at 529.6, 530.3, 531.7, and 534.5 eV; for K-ILT, at 529.7, 530.3, and 532.0 eV; and for Cs-ILT, at 530.0, 530.7, and 532.2 eV. From the low-energy side, these three O 1s peaks can be assigned to the 2-coordinated oxygen, the 4-coordinated oxygen, and water. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a> Meanwhile the fourth peak, at 534.5 eV in Na-ILT, is assigned to water in bilayer form. The formation of a bilayer hydrate in Na-ILT can also be evidenced by the XRD data of Na-ILT (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a).</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>A presents the FE-SEM pictures of Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT. All samples have a two-dimensional plate-like shape with a lateral size of ∼20 μm. The presence of plate-like crystals in all samples produced by the ion-exchange process implies that the layered structure is preserved. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>B shows the HR-TEM pictures of all samples. All materials in this work have parallel sheets, revealing that their layer-by-layer structures are well-ordered. In all of the samples, HR-TEM pictures show similar basal spacings, as determined by XRD findings. The maximum increase in the interlayer spacing of LTs is obtained by the intercalation of the largest-sized alkali-metal ions, indicating successful intercalation of alkali-metal ions. The ICP-MS results in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a> reveal the element information of the AMIILTs. The chemical formula of AMIILTs were calculated according to the results in the reference of Ti, i.e., Li<sub>0.74</sub>H<sub>0.33</sub>Ti<sub>1.73</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>0.53</sub>H<sub>0.54</sub>Ti<sub>1.73</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, K<sub>0.39</sub>H<sub>0.68</sub>Ti<sub>1.73</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and Cs<sub>0.49</sub>H<sub>0.58</sub>Ti<sub>1.73</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. Thus, Li-ILT &gt; Na-ILT &gt; Cs-ILT &gt; K-ILT was the order of alkaline metal ion concentrations in the AMIILT samples.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/medium/am2c15359_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (A) SEM and (B) HR-TEM pictures of (a) Li-ILT, (b) Na-ILT, (c) K-ILT, and (d) Cs-ILT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15359&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Element Analysis of AMIILTs</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"></colgroup><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">sample</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="2" align="left">Li-ILT</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="2" align="left">Na-ILT</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="2" align="left">K-ILT</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" colspan="2" align="left">Cs-ILT</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">element</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Li</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Ti</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Na</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Ti</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">K</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Ti</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Cs</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Ti</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">measured value (mg/g)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">25.14</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">404.04</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">52.88</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">361.69</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">71.52</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">387.22</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">240.84</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">303.57</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.2.  Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">A galvanostatic discharge–charge cycling test was used to investigate the effect of the guest ions of LTs on the SIB electrodes’ behavior. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a–d presents the potential profiles of Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT, which exhibit a gradual change in voltage as the number of inserting sodium ions increases. In the present materials, there is no evident plateau in K-ILT, revealing a capacitive-like behavior. The plateaus in Li-ILT, Na-ILT, and Cs-ILT present at 1.44 V for charge and 1.34 V for discharge indicate the clear presence of reversible redox reactions in the charging and discharging process. The CV curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) further explain this phenomenon. No obvious redox peaks are found for K-ILT, suggesting a small number of active sites in K-ILT. The redox process at 0.80 V during the first discharging process results from the irreversible generation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film due to electrolyte degradation and permanent entrapment of sodium ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> Among the materials tested, Na-ILT has a higher capacity for multiple initial cycles as compared to other materials. We speculate that the high alkaline metal ion concentration may be responsible for the high specific capacity of Na-ILT. Thus, the role of the alkaline metal ion concentration was evaluated, and the results are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. A clear trend of increasing specific capacity is observed as the alkaline metal ion concentration increases, confirming that a high alkaline metal ion concentration is helpful for improving the capacity.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/medium/am2c15359_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Voltage curves of the discharge and charge cycles of (a) Li-ILT, (b) Na-ILT, (c) K-ILT, and (d) Cs-ILT, with a current density of 0.1 A/g. (e) Calculated graph for the first cycle Coulombic efficiency of Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT. (f) Specific capacities and Coulombic efficiencies obtained with a current density of 0.1 A/g for Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15359&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Coulombic efficiencies in the first cycle for Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT are calculated to be 82.8, 80.2, 83.7, and 77.4%, respectively, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e. The formation of an unavoidable SEI due to electrolyte decomposition causes a decrease in the initial Coulombic efficiency. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> This trend is consistent with the first CV curves in Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), which show a cathodic peak at 0.91 V and a broad hump between 0.29 and 0.60, 0.55 and 0.92, and 0.65 and 0.97 V, respectively, in the initial charge process, and they are missing in the second cycle and onward. However, the Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT materials shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f have large Coulombic efficiencies of over 98% after their 8th, 11th, 8th, and 9th cycles, respectively, showing that all present samples have high reversibility of sodium-ion storage capacity after activation. The Coulombic efficiencies of all samples for the first 30 cycles are given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. Our Coulombic efficiency data are consistent with most of transition metal oxides, which exhibit a gradual rise in Coulombic efficiency. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 45 to 47">(45−47)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The charge and discharge capacities of the compounds in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f are significantly altered when the guest alkali-metal ions are changed, indicating the massive influence of interlayer ion species on the SIB anode performance. The specific capacity of Na-ILT for the 100th cycle is 137 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which is higher than those of Li-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT (95, 61, and 77 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively), indicating that the Na ion is best among all alkali-metal guest ions in the present study for enhancing the SIB performance. Moreover, all samples show good structural stability upon cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a> shows the rate performance of all samples. Under current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacities of Na-ILT are 153, 139, 120, 104, 81, and 48 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which are higher than those of all other samples. The improved rate performance even at a high current density further implies that the Na ion is the ideal guest ion for boosting the SIB electrode efficiency of LT in this study. We also tested the performance of these samples as the anodes of LIBs and potassium-ion batteries (KIBs) to determine the role of these alkaline metal ions in the corresponding battery, and the results are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. The results imply that selecting the corresponding ions is favorable for improving the performance of the batteries.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/medium/am2c15359_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Rate capacity graphs of Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT. The charge and discharge capacities are represented by the open and solid circles, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15359&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.3.  Mechanism Investigation</h3><div class="NLM_p">Since the intercalation of sodium ions into LT causes the redox mechanism of Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> coupling, we calculated the theoretical capacities as 210 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Li-ILT, 189 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Na-ILT, 172 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for K-ILT, and 112 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Cs-ILT considering all materials with the formula of AM<sub>1.07</sub>Ti<sub>1.73</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (AM = Li, Na, K, Cs). In terms of atomic weight, Li-ILT should deliver the highest specific capacity, which is not the case here. We have shown in the previous section that the alkaline metal ion concentrations are in the order Li-ILT &gt; Na-ILT &gt; Cs-ILT &gt; K-ILT. If we take the alkaline metal ion concentration into account, the theoretical capacities are 213 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Li-ILT, 203 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Na-ILT, 199 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for K-ILT, and 153 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Cs-ILT. Solely considering the mass of interlayer ions, again, Li-ILT should deliver the highest specific capacity and one should expect a similar specific capacity of K-ILT and Na-ILT. However, the Na-ILT sample delivers a much higher specific capacity than the K-ILT and Li-ILT samples. Thus, we believe that the atomic mass is not the dominating factor for the battery performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">To understand the electrochemical process in detail, we analyzed the CV graphs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) of all samples and created an association graph for the log of anodic/cathodic peak current versus sweep rate as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a> to further investigate the influence of alkali-metal ions on the electrochemical reaction of LT. As the role of a charge-transfer-based as well as a diffusion-based mechanism can be distinguished from the variation of peak current with scan rate, the major electrochemical processes responsible for the electrode activity of these substances can be calculated from the correlation plots of <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, where <i>i</i> represents the current, <i>v</i> represents the scanning speed, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a> The charge-transfer-based process has a <i>b</i> value of 1, while the diffusion-based electrochemical process has a <i>b</i> value of 0.5. The charge-transfer mechanism is linked to a capacitive mechanism induced when Na ions touch the surface of anode materials, whereas the diffusion-based process is related to the exchange process of Na ions in the interlayer of layered materials. The <i>b</i> values of the first anodic and cathodic peaks are 0.84, 0.63, 0.88, and 0.82 and 0.84, 0.78, 0.96, and 0.90 for Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT, respectively, based on the current CV curves obtained at various scan rates (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). The values for Na-ILT obtained in this study are similar to the values for layered lepidocrocite-type sodium titanate reported elsewhere. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> These findings reveal that the electrochemical activities of all samples are influenced by both diffusion and a charge-transfer-based mechanism. However, except for Na-ILT, the capacitive mechanism contributes more in the other samples, indicating that more active sites between layers can be utilized via diffusion in Na-ILT, while in the other samples, most of the active sites are located on the surface. These data can explain well the relatively larger capacity of Na-ILT compared to those of the others.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/medium/am2c15359_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. The log of peak current versus log of scan rate for the (a) first anodic peak and (b) first cathodic peak for Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15359&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">EIS measurements were carried out to acquire more details. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a> shows the EIS results of the current materials obtained before and after 100 electrochemical charge–discharge cycles. The charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of the electrode/electrolyte interface is represented by the diameter of the semicircle in Nyquist plots. In contrast, the Na-ion absorption capacity inside the electrode is shown by the low-frequency impedance (straight line). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref52 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51 ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 51 to 53">(51−53)</a> The current EIS data were evaluated by an equivalent circuit (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>) consisting of a charge-transfer and SEI layer diffusion component, in order to obtain quantitative details on the kinetic factors of the electrode mechanism.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/medium/am2c15359_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Nyquist plots of Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT (a) before and (b) after 100 electrochemical cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15359&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Na-ILT and K-ILT have lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values and sharper straight-line slopes than Li-ILT and Cs-ILT before the electrochemical cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), revealing a lowered <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value and an increased sodium-ion absorption. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of Na-ILT remained almost stable, while the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of Li-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT nanocomposites significantly decreased after 100 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). The decreases in the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of Li-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT nanocomposites also justify their surface capacitive response in the CV curves. The value of the SEI layer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>) of Na-ILT is very small as compared to those of other samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), revealing that sodium ions easily travel into Na-ILT through the SEI layer compared to the other samples. The large <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> values of Li-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT suggest that a harmful SEI layer is formed in these compounds. The very large SEI values of K-ILT and Cs-ILT suggest that a too large interlayer spacing may facilitate the formation of SEI layers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 54 and 55">(54,55)</a> The harmful SEI layer may come from alloying of Na and other alkaline metal ions, but it has to be verified.</div><div class="NLM_p">The diffusion coefficient <i>D</i> can be calculated from the EIS curves according to the equation below: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57 ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56 ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 56 to 58">(56−58)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-21"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-22">2</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-23"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-24" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-25">A</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-26" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-27">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-28"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-29" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-30">n</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-31" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-32">4</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-33"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-34" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-35">F</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-36" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-37">4</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-38"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-39" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-40">C</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-41" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-42">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-43"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-44" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-45">σ</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-46" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-47">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>D</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><msup><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>T</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><msup><mrow><mi>A</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>F</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>C</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>σ</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">In the above equation, the gas constant, absolute temperature, surface area, number of electrons per molecule that are transferred, Faraday’s constant, the concentration of Na ions, and the Warburg factor are represented by <i>R</i>, <i>T</i>, <i>A</i>, <i>n</i>, <i>F</i>, <i>C</i>, and σ, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> The Nyquist diagram is used to extract the data in the low-frequency range, and the Warburg factor σ is calculated by graphing <i>Z</i>′ versus ω<sup>–1/2</sup> before cycling and after 100 cycles, as illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>. The diffusion coefficients <i>D</i> of Li-ILT, Na-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT are 2.95 × 10<sup>–14</sup>, 3.16 × 10<sup>–14</sup>, 1.81 × 10<sup>–14</sup>, and 4.81 × 10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, before cycling, and the values are similar. However, they change to 11.0 × 10<sup>–14</sup>, 5.3 × 10<sup>–14</sup>, 23.3 × 10<sup>–14</sup>, and 25.2 × 10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/suppl_file/am2c15359_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1 and S2</a>), and the Li-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT samples show significantly increased values after cycling, a process in which the SEI layer forms. The significant increase of the diffusion coefficient can be explained by their capacitive ion storage mechanism demonstrated by the above <i>b</i> values. It seems that the diffusion channels in the Li-ILT, K-ILT, and Cs-ILT samples have been greatly blocked after cycling, so these samples exhibited a small specific capacity, despite the high ion diffusivity.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition to the experimental investigation, we also performed a theoretical calculation to understand the Na-ion diffusion between layers in different compounds. Lepidocrocite-type titanate has been demonstrated to have a preferential diffusion path along the <i>c</i> axis, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> so we examined the sodium-ion diffusion along the <i>c</i> axis in different compounds. The diffusion paths we examined here are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a,b. First, the formation energy along different diffusion paths is calculated and the results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c,d. The formation energy of diffusion via path 1 is negative, but the formation energy of diffusion path 2 is positive, indicating that diffusion path 2 is thermodynamically unfavorable. These results have motivated us to focus on the study of sodium-ion diffusion along diffusion path 1. The diffusion energy along the path 1 is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a, and the energy maxima occur when the sodium ions jump across the alkaline metal ions. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b exhibits that the diffusion energies are in the order Cs-ILT &gt; Li-ILT &gt; Na-ILT &gt; K-ILT, suggesting that sodium-ion diffusion is more difficult in Cs-ILT. Surprisingly, the diffusion energy in K-ILT, similar to that in Na-ILT, is the lowest, suggesting that K-ILT is a potential candidate as an anode material provided that the negative effect of the SEI layer is eliminated.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/medium/am2c15359_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Schematic demonstration of sodium-ion diffusion in the two different paths investigated, (a) path 1 and (b) path 2, and their formation energy at starting and ending points (c) for path 1 and (d) path 2. The distance in panels (c) and (d) follows the coordinate in the unit cell along the <i>c</i> axis.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15359&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/medium/am2c15359_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) Diffusion energy of sodium ions along path 1 and (b) max energy in different compounds.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15359/asset/images/large/am2c15359_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15359&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">To summarize, interlayer engineering by intercalation for ionic LT is an effective way to optimize its performance as an anode material for SIBs. However, the role of the intercalating ions has to be carefully considered.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In conclusion, we have studied AMIILTs (AM = Li, Na, K, Cs) obtained by a simple solution processing approach for their application as SIB electrodes to verify the role of alkaline ion species. We found that sodium ions are the most suitable guest ions for attaining the highest SIB electrochemical performance of LTs among the various alkali-metal ions. The higher specific capacity of Na-ILT compared to those of others is attributed to its high Na concentration and efficient utilization of the active sites that are accessible by diffusion. Our DFT calculation reveals that although Cs-ILT has the largest interlayer spacing, the diffusion energy of sodium ions jumping across Cs ions is the largest, which hampers sodium-ion diffusion. K-ILT and Na-ILT hold much less diffusion energy. Thus, for ionic layered materials, not only is the interlayer spacing a significant factor, but also the guest ion species should be carefully considered.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i15">Supporting                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600943"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, M.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synergetic stability enhancement with magnesium and calcium ion substitution for Ni/Mn-based P2-type sodium-ion battery cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fu, Hongwei; Wang, Yun-Peng; Fan, Guozheng; Guo, Shan; Xie, Xuesong; Cao, Xinxin; Lu, Bingan; Long, Mengqiu; Zhou, Jiang; Liang, Shuquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">726-736</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSHCCN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-6520</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The conventional P2-type cathode material Na0.67Ni0.33Mn0.67O2 suffers from an irreversible P2-O2 phase transition and serious capacity fading during cycling.  Here, we successfully carry out magnesium and calcium ion doping into the transition-metal layers (TM layers) and the alkali-metal layers (AM layers), resp., of Na0.67Ni0.33Mn0.67O2.  Both Mg and Ca doping can reduce O-type stacking in the high-voltage region, leading to enhanced cycling endurance, however, this is assocd. with a decrease in capacity.  The results of d. functional theory (DFT) studies reveal that the introduction of Mg2+ and Ca2+ make high-voltage reactions (oxygen redox and Ni4+/Ni3+ redox reactions) less accessible.  Thanks to the synergetic effect of co-doping with Mg2+ and Ca2+ ions, the adverse effects on high-voltage reactions involving Ni-O bonding are limited, and the structural stability is further enhanced.  The finally obtained P2-type Na0.62Ca0.025Ni0.28Mg0.05Mn0.67O2 exhibits a satisfactory initial energy d. of 468.2 W h kg-1 and good capacity retention of 83% after 100 cycles at 50 mA g-1 within the voltage range of 2.2-4.35 V.  This work deepens our understanding of the specific effects of Mg2+ and Ca2+ dopants and provides a stability-enhancing strategy utilizing abundant alk. earth elements.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b07031"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Piao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oh, S.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Critical Role of the Chemical Environment of Interlayer Na Sites: An Effective Way To Improve the Na Ion Electrode Activity of Layered Titanate</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Piao, Meina; Oh, Seung Mi; Lim, Joohyun; Kim, Sun Ha; Kim, Sung-Chul; Jo, Yun Kyung; Han, Oc Hee; Hwang, Seong-Ju</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">39</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">33112-33123</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The chem. environments of the interlayer Na sites of layered titanate are finely controlled by the intercalation of n-alkylamine with various alkyl chain lengths to explore an effective way to improve its electrode functionality for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  The n-alkylamine intercalation via ion-exchange and exfoliation-restacking routes allows the modification of in-plane structures of layered titanate to be tuned.  Among the present n-alkylamine-intercalates, the n-pentylamine-intercalated titanate shows the largest discharge capacity with the best rate characteristics, underscoring the crit. role of optimized intracryst. structure in improving the SIB electrode performance of layered titanate.  The creation of turbostratic in-plane structure degrades the SIB electrode performance of layered titanate, indicating the detrimental effect of in-plane structural disorder on electrode activity. 23Na magic-angle spinning NMR spectroscopy demonstrates that the n-alkylamine-intercalated titanates possess two different interlayer Na+ sites near ammonium head groups/titanate layers and near alkyl chains.  The intercalation of long-chain mols. increases the population of the latter site and the overall mobility of Na+ ions, which is responsible for the improvement of electrode activity upon n-alkylamine intercalation.  The present study highlights that the increased population of interlayer metal sites remote from the host layers is effective in improving the electrode functionality of layered metal oxide for SIBs and multivalent ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1107/S0567739476001551"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shannon, R.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhancing sodium-ion battery performance with interlayer-expanded MoS2-PEO nanocomposites</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yifei; Liang, Yanliang; Robles Hernandez, Francisco C.; Deog Yoo, Hyun; An, Qinyou; Yao, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The larger ionic radius of Na ion (1.06 Å) compared with that of Li ion (0.76 Å) is a fundamental reason for the inferior diffusion kinetics of Na ion in intercalation hosts.  Here we report interlayer expansion of intercalation hosts as a general strategy to facilitate the solid-state diffusion of Na ions.  Based on this strategy, poly(ethylene oxide)-intercalated MoS2 composites (PEO-MoS2) were synthesized via a facile exfoliation-restacking method and tested as anode materials for Na-ion batteries (NIBs).  The interlayer spacing of MoS2 was increased from 0.615 nm to 1.45 nm by insertion of controlled amts. of PEO.  The bilayer PEO-intercalated MoS2 composite (PEO2L-MoS2) exhibits a specific capacity of 225 mA h g-1 under a c.d. of 50 mA g-1, twice as high as that of com. MoS2 (com-MoS2), and shows improved rate performance due to enhanced Na-ion diffusivity.  The improvement in the electrochem. performance demonstrates that interlayer expansion is an effective strategy for the development of high performance electrode materials for battery technologies based on the storage of large ions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202001708"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ang, E.                 10.1002/adfm.202001708</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D30%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D2001708%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26author%3DB.%2BWang%26author%3DE.%2BH.%2BAng%26author%3DY.%2BYang%26author%3DY.%2BZhang%26author%3DH.%2BGeng%26author%3DM.%2BYe%26author%3DC.%2BC.%2BLi%26title%3DInterlayer%2BEngineering%2Bof%2BMolybdenum%2BTrioxide%2Btoward%2BHigh-Capacity%2Band%2BStable%2BSodium%2BIon%2BHalf%252FFull%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.202001708&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c15359&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.202001708&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interlayer Engineering of Molybdenum Trioxide toward High-Capacity and Stable Sodium Ion Half/Full Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Bo; Ang, Edison Huixiang; Yang, Yang; Zhang, Yufei; Geng, Hongbo; Ye, Minghui; Li, Cheng Chao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">28</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2001708</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Orthorhombic molybdenum trioxide (MoO3) is one of the most promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries because of its rich chem. assocd. with multiple valence states and intriguing layered structure.  However, MoO3 still suffers from the low rate capability and poor cycle induced by pulverization during de/sodiation.  An ingenious two-step synthesis strategy to fine tune the layer structure of MoO3 targeting stable and fast sodium ionic diffusion channels is reported here.  By integrating partially redn. and org. mol. intercalation methodologies, the interlayer spacing of MoO3 is remarkably enlarged to 10.40 Å and the layer structural integration are reinforced by dimercapto groups of bismuththiol mols.  Comprehensive characterizations and d. functional theory calcns. prove that the intercalated bismuththiol (DMcT) mols. substantially enhanced electronic cond. and effectively shield the electrostatic interaction between Na+ and the MoO3 host by conjugated double bond, resulting in improved Na+ insertion/extn. kinetics.  Benefiting from these features, the newly devised layered MoO3 electrode achieves excellent long-term cycling stability and outstanding rate performance.  These achievements are of vital significance for the prepn. of sodium-ion battery anode materials with high-rate capability and long cycling life using intercalation chem.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201604108"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">S-Doped N-Rich Carbon Nanosheets with Expanded Interlayer Distance as Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, Yang; He, Kai; Zhu, Yujie; Han, Fudong; Xu, Yunhua; Matsuda, Isamu; Ishii, Yoshitaka; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4033</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.0c10237"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lü, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synergistically Controlled Mechanism of Sodium Birnessite with a Larger Interlayer Distance for Fast Ion Intercalation toward Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synergistically Controlled Mechanism of Sodium Birnessite with a Larger Interlayer Distance for Fast Ion Intercalation toward Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Fanghui; Zeng, Shuyi; Duan, Lianfeng; Zhang, Xueyu; Li, Xuesong; Wang, Liying; Yang, Xijia; Lu, Wei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The high theor. capacity of sodium birnessite cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) has been restricted due to drastic vol. variation and fast capacity fading.  The underlying reasons are the interlayer electrostatic interaction and the Mn3+ Jahn-Teller effect.  To overcome these two effects, herein, the synergistically controlled mechanism of sodium birnessite with a larger interlayer distance is achieved through enhancing the interlayer distance.  Tetramethylammonium ions (TMA+) as effective supporters stabilized the host structure, providing a fast and stable Na+ transmission channel.  The TMA+ intercalated into sodium birnessite as a cathode material in sodium-ion batteries delivered a specific discharge capacity of 136 mAh g-1 at 2C and a Coulombic efficiency of 100% after 200 cycles.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effective Interlayer Engineering of Two-Dimensional VOPO4 Nanosheets via Controlled Organic Intercalation for Improving Alkali Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peng, Lele; Zhu, Yue; Peng, Xu; Fang, Zhiwei; Chu, Wangsheng; Wang, Yu; Xie, Yujun; Li, Yafei; Cha, Judy J.; Yu, Guihua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
